.L 4
.S Nominations open for Homewood Council on Inclusive Excellence
.F Ed Schlesinger and Beverly Wendland <wsecommunications@jhu.edu>
.D Tue, 14 May 2019 13:04:40 +0000
.M
Nominations may be submitted through May 20.

Homewood Council on Inclusive Excellence
View this email in your browser (https://mailchi.mp/jhu/19-nom-hcie?e=a57d9a1452)
Message from the Dean
Dear Homewood faculty, students, and staff,

“Inclusive excellence” is the idea that we are at our very best when we abide by our core values in diversity, inclusion and equity. Here on the Homewood campus, we strive for a dynamic culture that enables full participation of each member of our community in research and education. In research, we arrive at better and more relevant solutions when we have input from people with different backgrounds, and in education, our inclusion serves to help create a diverse group of future leaders for our society.

The Homewood Council for Inclusive Excellence (http://hcie.jhu.edu)  (HCIE) advises us on matters of diversity, advocates for policies that foster an inclusive climate, identifies current practices that pose barriers to the engagement and success of underrepresented constituents, increases awareness of best practices, and implements innovations that advance our diversity goals outlined in the university’s Roadmap on Diversity and Inclusion (http://web.jhu.edu/administration/provost/diversity_roadmap) . The Council is led by KSAS Professor Rigoberto Hernandez and WSE Professor Jeff Gray and the Assistant Dean for Diversity and Inclusion, Darlene Saporu.

Over the past two years, the HCIE’s recommendations led to a pilot faculty mentoring workshop and new diversity modules for graduate student TA orientation. They engaged in discussions with campus leaders on admissions, family leave policies, and faculty hiring, and they provided input to us on emerging issues such as the campus police force.
To continue this work, we are seeking nominations of students, staff, and faculty (https://forms.gle/2a4TkKRwknTZ48rz5)  to serve on the council starting in the fall of 2019. Members serve terms of one to three years and commit to work with fellow HCIE members and stakeholders across campus. The Council strives to include a broad coalition of members representing campus constituencies. Self-nominations are accepted. We ask you to consider serving or nominating others who would contribute toward our mission of inclusive excellence on the Homewood Campus. Nominations may be submitted through May 20.

Sincerely,
Ed Schlesinger
Benjamin T. Rome Dean
Whiting School of Engineering
Beverly Wendland
James B. Knapp Dean
Krieger School of Arts and Sciences

1st annual Student Poster Session on Diversity & Inclusion (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/05/15/1st-annual-student-poster-session-on-diversity-inclusion/)
When: Wednesday, 3:30-5pm
Where: Charles Commons, Homewood Campus
Summary: The Johns Hopkins Diversity Leadership Council announces the 1st annual Student Poster Session on Diversity & Inclusion to be held at the Johns Hopkins Diversity Awards Ceremony on May 15, 2019. All JHU students (undergraduate and graduate) are invited to submit posters. Posters may be on any diversity-related topic that is associated with student life, academics, the Johns Hopkins organization, the surrounding communities, the city of Baltimore, or the state of Maryland. Suggested topics include: anti-Muslim sentiment, dismantling institutional racism, racial and religious diversity, Latinx inclusion, accessibility, gender equity in STEM, gender gap in higher ed, LGBTQ issues, community engagement, and immigrant populations. Three awards of $500 will be given to the authors of the best posters. Poster abstract submission deadline: April 12, 2019.

Symposium: "Advancing the German-American Alliance: Updating the Transatlantic Partnership for the Next 70 Years" (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/05/15/advancing-the-german-american-alliance-updating-the-transatlantic-partnership-for-the-next-70-years/)
When: Wednesday, 10am-3pm
Where: Allianz Forum, Pariser Platz 6, 10117 Berlin, Germany
Summary: The year 2019 marks several major anniversaries that trace the arc of the transatlantic relationship. This half-day symposium, hosted by the Johns Hopkins American Institute for Contemporary German Studies and the European Council on Foreign Relations, will examine the challenges facing the transatlantic community and offer a vision for the alliance in the decades ahead.

.I 5
.L 4
.S Melissa Hyatt stepping down as VP of security
.F Senior Vice President Ennis and Senior Vice President Kasdin <jhucommunications@johnshopkins.edu>
.D Fri, 10 May 2019 14:45:44 +0000
.M
Is this email not displaying correctly? View it in your browser (https://mailchi.mp/df8e202efcc9/melissa-hyatt-stepping-down-as-vp-of-security?e=278d787a13) .
LINK
Dear Students, Faculty, and Staff:

As you may know, on Tuesday, Baltimore County Executive Johnny Olszewski announced his nomination of Melissa Hyatt as Baltimore County police chief. Melissa will officially step down from her role at Johns Hopkins in the coming weeks.

Over the past year as vice president for security for Johns Hopkins Medicine and Johns Hopkins University, Melissa has led the security operations of our institution with dedicated leadership and strong expertise. She has worked diligently to maintain the safety of our students, faculty, staff, clinicians, employees, patients, and visitors. While we are saddened by Melissa’s departure, we are grateful for her service and wish her well in her new role.

We are carefully considering the transition plan and will announce an interim leadership plan soon. Please join us in thanking Melissa for her leadership and service to JHM and JHU.

Sincerely,

Daniel G. Ennis
Senior Vice President for Finance and Administration
Johns Hopkins University

Robert Kasdin
Senior Vice President, Chief Financial Officer and Chief Operating Officer
Johns Hopkins Medicine

Thirty-two Hopkins PhD students transformed years of thesis work into three-minute presentations for the fourth-annual Three-Minute Thesis competition last month. Eduardo Martinez-Montes (right), who studies biochemistry and cellular and molecular biology at the School of Medicine, won with a talk that compared the body's DNA to a library and his field of epigenetics to how a library is organized.

------------------------------------------------------------

This workshop, hosted by the Professional Development and Career Office (https://jhu.joinhandshake.com/events/305079/share_preview?utm_source=Masters+and+grad+students+digest+March+2019&utm_campaign=527a44c9bb-EMAIL_CAMPAIGN_2019_04_05_08_06&utm_medium=email&utm_term=0_c3052827b1-527a44c9bb-70387941&mc_cid=527a44c9bb&mc_eid=[UNIQID]) , will highlight statistics on what careers Johns Hopkins alumni are in and what careers PhD scientists nationally have pursued. .I 10
.L 4
.F "Randal Burns, Chair" <chairadm@cs.jhu.edu>
.S Mourning the loss of a student in Computer Science
.D Thu, 9 May 2019 11:11:04 -0400
.M
Dear Computer Science Students, Faculty, and Staff,

It is with deep sadness that we write to inform you of the loss of one of
our students, Tianshi Liao, a computer science master's student in the
Whiting School of Engineering. Tianshi, who was from Guangzhou, China,
passed away on Friday, May 3. It was an accidental death and there is no
indication of any community safety concerns related to his passing.

Campus administrators have been in contact with his family to provide
support during this difficult time, and we convey our heartfelt condolences
to his family and friends for their terrible loss. Please keep them in your
thoughts in the days ahead.

Tianshi earned a bachelor of science in chemical engineering from China
University of Petroleum and a master of science in chemical engineering from
the University of Massachusetts, Lowell. Last month, he submitted a paper
entitled "Hierarchical Structural-Aware Deep Supervision" to the
International Conference on Computer Vision.  

His colleagues remember him as a team player, willing to help others when
needed, and a hard worker who developed his own ideas.  Tianshi was an
engaged athlete, enjoying tennis and badminton, and volunteering at the 2008
Olympic Games in Beijing.  

If you would like to express your condolences to the family, you may write
an email or letter to "The Family of Tianshi Liao" and send it to
<mailto:drobin79@jhu.edu> Diedre Robinson in the Whiting School of
Engineering Dean's Office, who will collect these messages on the family's
behalf.

When we lose a member of our community, some may find themselves in need of
support. Students may reach out to the Homewood Counseling Center at
410-516-8278. Outside of normal hours, the counselor on call may be reached
through Security at 410-516-7777. 

For members of our faculty and staff who are looking for support, the
Faculty and Staff Assistance Program (FASAP) provides private and
confidential services free of charge, including assessment, diagnosis, and
referral by FASAP clinicians; crisis intervention; brief treatment; and
training, education, and outreach. Please visit the
<http://jhu.us6.list-manage.com/track/click?u=40512314224886c4ca8b856c2&id=1
a24eec49f&e=53c58a9b08> FASAP website for additional information. 

Together, we mourn the loss of such a promising member of our community.

Sincerely,

T.E. "Ed" Schlesinger
Benjamin T. Rome Dean
G.W.C. Whiting School of Engineering

Kevin G. Shollenberger
Vice Provost for Student Affairs

May 6, 2019 Powered by the Hub

Announcements for students on JHU’s Homewood campus

Announcements

Top Announcements

Space@Hopkins call for 2019 seed grant proposals (https://hub.jhu.edu/announcements/2019/02/01/space-at-hopkins-seed-grants/)
Grants of up to $25,000 for one year are available for space-related projects; applications are due May 8

Research Participants Wanted

Healthy participants needed for paid computer task research study (https://hub.jhu.edu/announcements/2019/03/21/healthy-participants-needed-for-paid-computer-task-research-study/)
Participation in the study, held at the East Baltimore campus, takes about 30 minutes; compensation $25/hour

Upcoming Events

Arts+Culture

Movements: Black Print Culture in the United States, 1773–1940 (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/02/11/black-print-culture/)
When: Feb 11 - May 24, 12am
Where: MSE Library, Homewood Campus
Summary: An exhibition exploring the circulation and contribution of black print culture through original items drawn from the Sheridan Libraries Special Collections.

First Monday Coffeehouse (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/05/06/first-monday-coffeehouse/)
When: Today, 5-7pm
Where: Preclinical Teaching Building, East Baltimore Campus
Summary: May's guest performers are Phillip McKnight and friends, playing traditional music from North America and the Caribbean.

Exhibition: The Photographer's Book (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/05/06/exhibition-the-photographers-book/)
When: Today, 6-8pm
Where: Brody Learning Commons, Homewood Campus
Summary: View ten handmade artist books created by students enrolled in The Photographer's Book. The students received guidance from photographer Phyllis Berger and artist book designer Betty Sweren. Each book illustrates a favorite piece of text, with the subjects ranging from poetry and song lyrics to a play or narrative.

Elizabeth Futral, soprano (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/05/06/peabody-elizabeth-futral-soprano/)
When: Today, 7:30-9:30pm
Where: Leith Symington Griswold Hall, Peabody Institute
Summary: Faculty soprano performs works she has premiered over her career.

Sylvia Adalman Chamber Series

Tickets Available: April 1

Student Life

Glasses Drive (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/05/01/glasses-drive/)
When: May 1 - May 14, 12am
Where: Charles Commons, Homewood Campus
Summary: Support equal access to eye health and donate your old sunglasses and eyeglasses, as well as cases and accessories, at the drop box at the Johns Hopkins Rec Center Welcome Desk.

Dig Deep Volleyball Tournament (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/05/06/dig-deep-volleyball-tournament/)
When: Today, 11am-2pm
Where: Athletic Circle, Homewood Campus
Summary: Dig Deep is an annual volleyball tournament to raise awareness about mental health resources on campus, decrease the stigma around mental health conversations among college students, and raise money for the JED Foundation. No volleyball experience necessary.

Whiting School's Convocation Awards Ceremony (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/05/06/whiting-schools-convocation-awards-ceremony/)
When: Today, 3-5pm
Where: Shriver Hall, Homewood Campus
Summary: The Whiting School's annual Convocation Awards Ceremony is a celebration of student and faculty achievement at the Whiting School of Engineering. The event is open to award recipients, their family and friends, and members of the Whiting School community.

Engineering Design Day 2019 (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/05/07/jhu-design-day-2019/)
When: Tuesday, 9am-6pm
Where: Glass Pavilion, Homewood Campus
Summary: Through poster sessions, presentations, and prototype demonstrations, Johns Hopkins engineers will demonstrate their ability to apply their knowledge and skills to tackle real-world challenges. Follow on social media at #JHUDesignDay.

Wellness Fitness Series: Outdoor Nia Class (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/05/07/uhs-wellness-fitness-series-outdoor-nia-class/)
When: Tuesday, 5:30-6:30pm
Where: East Baltimore Campus
Summary: Join University Health Services Wellness this spring and summer for their popular free fitness series, including outdoor Nia.

Lectures+Symposiums

Hopkins Extreme Materials Institute Seminar: Maryam Ghazisaeidi (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/05/06/hemi-seminar-maryam-ghazisaeidi/)
When: Today, 11am-12pm
Where: Malone Hall, Homewood Campus
Summary: The Hopkins Extreme Materials Institute is proud to present Dr. Maryam Ghazisaeidi, an assistant professor Materials Science Engineering and Physics at the Ohio State University, as part of its 2018-19 Lecture Series. Dr. Ghazisaeidi will present on the topic "High Entropy Alloys: Mechanical Properties and Phase Stability."

Center for Language and Speech Processing Spring Seminar Series: Kenneth Heafield (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/05/06/center-for-language-and-speech-processing-spring-seminar-series-kenneth-heafield/)
When: Today, 12-1:15pm
Where: Hackerman Hall, Homewood Campus
Summary: Dr. Kenneth Heafield, an assistant professor in the School of Informatics at the University of Edinburgh, will present a Center for Language and Speech Processing seminar titled "Faster Neural Machine Translation."

Baltimore's Billie Holiday: A Journey in Maps & Archives (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/05/06/baltimores-billie-holiday-a-journey-in-maps-archives/)
When: Today, 3:30-5pm
Where: Brody Learning Commons, Homewood Campus
Summary: The Billie Holiday Project for Liberation Arts at Johns Hopkins University is pleased to present "Baltimore's Billie Holiday: A Journey in Maps & Archives," a lecture with Dr. Lawrence Jackson, a Bloomberg Distinguished Professor of English and History, and students Francisco Perez Marsilla and Bianca Martone.

2019 Passano Award Lecture: Robert Fettiplace (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/05/06/the-2019-passano-award-lecture-with-dr-robert-fettiplace/)
When: Today, 4-5pm
Where: Preclinical Teaching Building, East Baltimore Campus
Summary: Dr. Robert Fettiplace, a professor of Neural and Behavioral Sciences at the University of Wisconsin, will present the 2019 Passano Award Lecture entitled "The Molecular Make-Up of the Hair Cell Transduction Channel" for the Department of Molecular Biology & Genetics.

Isaac and Leah M. Potts Memorial Lecture: Roslyn Weiss (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/05/06/the-isaac-and-leah-m-potts-memorial-lecture/)
When: Today, 5-7pm
Where: Gilman Hall, Homewood Campus
Summary: Dr. Roslyn Weiss, a professor of Ancient Philosophy and Medieval Jewish Philosophy at Lehigh University, will give the Isaac and Leah M. Potts Memorial Lecture for the Program in Jewish Studies.

Engineering Design Day 2019 (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/05/07/jhu-design-day-2019/)
When: Tuesday, 9am-6pm
Where: Glass Pavilion, Homewood Campus
Summary: Through poster sessions, presentations, and prototype demonstrations, Johns Hopkins engineers will demonstrate their ability to apply their knowledge and skills to tackle real-world challenges. Follow on social media at #JHUDesignDay.

Dept. of Biological Chemistry Tuesday Seminar Series: Eric Klein (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/05/07/the-department-of-biological-chemistry-tuesday-seminar-series-eric-klein/)
When: Tuesday, 12-1pm
Where: Physiology Building, East Baltimore Campus
Summary: Dr. Eric Klein, an assistant professor of Biology at Rutgers University-Camden, will give a talk entitled "Adaptation to Phosphate Limitation in Caulobacter crescentus" for the Department of Biological Chemistry.

.F Reatha Holt <rholt6@jhu.edu>
.S CS Seminar Speaker: Vishal Patel– "Deep Networks for Open Set Visual Recognition"
.D Thu, 2 May 2019 12:53:30 +0000
.M
Speaker:  Vishal Patel, Johns Hopkins University
Date & Time: May 2, 2019 at 10:30 am
Location: Hackerman B17
Title: Deep Networks for Open Set Visual Recognition

Abstract:

Over the last five years, methods based on Deep Convolutional Neural Networks (DCNNs) have shown impressive performance improvements for object detection and recognition problems. This has been made possible due to the availability of large annotated datasets, a better understanding of the non-linear mapping between input images and class labels as well as the affordability of GPUs. However, a vast majority of DCNN-based recognition methods are designed for a closed world, where the primary assumption is that all categories are known a priori. In many real-world applications, this assumption does not necessarily hold. In this talk, I will present some of my recent works on developing DCNN-based algorithms for open set recognition as well as novelty detection and one-class recognition. I will conclude my talk by describing several promising directions for future research.
Bio:


Vishal M. Patel is an Assistant Professor in the Department of Electrical and Computer Engineering (ECE) at Johns Hopkins University. Prior to joining Hopkins, he was an A. Walter Tyson Assistant Professor in the Department of ECE at Rutgers University and a member of the research faculty at the University of Maryland Institute for Advanced Computer Studies (UMIACS). He completed his Ph.D. in Electrical Engineering from the University of Maryland, College Park, MD, in 2010. He has received a number of awards including the 2016 ONR Young Investigator Award, the 2016 Jimmy Lin Award for Invention, A. Walter Tyson Assistant Professorship Award, Best Paper Award at IEEE AVSS 2017, Best Paper Award at IEEE BTAS 2015, Honorable Mention Paper Award at IAPR ICB 2018, two Best Student Paper Awards at IAPR ICPR 2018, and Best Poster Awards at BTAS 2015 and 2016. He is an Associate Editor of the IEEE Signal Processing Magazine and serves on the Information Forensics and Security Technical Committee of the IEEE Signal Processing Society. He is serving as the  Vice  President (Conferences)  of  the  IEEE  Biometrics  Council.  He is a member of Eta Kappa Nu, Pi Mu Epsilon, and Phi Beta Kappa.


Reatha Holt
Sr. Administrative Coordinator
Johns Hopkins University
Computer Science Department
Malone 160
410-516-8775

.F Reatha Holt <rholt6@jhu.edu>
.S  CS Seminar Speaker: Vishal Patel– "Deep Networks for Open Set Visual Recognition"
.D Fri, 26 Apr 2019 18:33:08 +0000
.M
Speaker:  Vishal Patel, Johns Hopkins University
Date & Time: May 2, 2019 at 10:30 am
Location: Hackerman B17
Title: Deep Networks for Open Set Visual Recognition

Abstract:

Over the last five years, methods based on Deep Convolutional Neural Networks (DCNNs) have shown impressive performance improvements for object detection and recognition problems. This has been made possible due to the availability of large annotated datasets, a better understanding of the non-linear mapping between input images and class labels as well as the affordability of GPUs. However, a vast majority of DCNN-based recognition methods are designed for a closed world, where the primary assumption is that all categories are known a priori. In many real-world applications, this assumption does not necessarily hold. In this talk, I will present some of my recent works on developing DCNN-based algorithms for open set recognition as well as novelty detection and one-class recognition. I will conclude my talk by describing several promising directions for future research.

Bio:


Vishal M. Patel is an Assistant Professor in the Department of Electrical and Computer Engineering (ECE) at Johns Hopkins University. Prior to joining Hopkins, he was an A. Walter Tyson Assistant Professor in the Department of ECE at Rutgers University and a member of the research faculty at the University of Maryland Institute for Advanced Computer Studies (UMIACS). He completed his Ph.D. in Electrical Engineering from the University of Maryland, College Park, MD, in 2010. He has received a number of awards including the 2016 ONR Young Investigator Award, the 2016 Jimmy Lin Award for Invention, A. Walter Tyson Assistant Professorship Award, Best Paper Award at IEEE AVSS 2017, Best Paper Award at IEEE BTAS 2015, Honorable Mention Paper Award at IAPR ICB 2018, two Best Student Paper Awards at IAPR ICPR 2018, and Best Poster Awards at BTAS 2015 and 2016. He is an Associate Editor of the IEEE Signal Processing Magazine and serves on the Information Forensics and Security Technical Committee of the IEEE Signal Processing Society. He is serving as the  Vice  President (Conferences)  of  the  IEEE  Biometrics  Council.  He is a member of Eta Kappa Nu, Pi Mu Epsilon, and Phi Beta Kappa.


Reatha Holt
Sr. Administrative Coordinator
Johns Hopkins University
Computer Science Department
Malone 160
410-516-8775



.I 34
.L 4
.F Reatha Holt <rholt6@jhu.edu>
.S Student Defense: Disa Mhembere, Johns Hopkins University - April 29 ,2019-11:00 AM
.D Fri, 26 Apr 2019 12:43:58 +0000
.M
Ph.D. Candidate: Disa Mhembere

Data & Time: Monday, April 29, 2019 at 11:00 AM - 1:00 PM

Location: Malone 228

Title: Scalable Graph Analysis and Clustering on Commodity Hardware

Abstract:
Today's large-scale datasets necessitate scalable data analysis frameworks and libraries. Traditional distributed memory solutions neglect optimizations for prevalent Non-Uniform Memory Access (NUMA) architectures. Additionally, distributed memory solutions often solely rely on process-level parallelism and thus forgo shared and external memory optimizations, leading to suboptimal overall performance. This thesis explores the effects of NUMA-awareness and fine-grain I/O optimizations from SSDs to improve hardware minimality, scalability and memory parallelism in graph analytics and community detection. Our computation optimizations target data that reside either (i) in-memory, (ii) semi-externally, or (iii) in distributed memory. We first present Graphyti, a semi-external memory graph library built on the FlashGraph framework to demonstrate key design principles for vertex-centric, semi-external memory (SEM) graph applications. Graphyti on a single thick node achieves comparable performance to popular distributed graph libraries in a cluster. We then address web-scale community detection and present the clusterNOR framework. We advance the state-of-the-art for memory parallel NUMA computation of k-means and subsequently clustering algorithms that follow the Majorize-Minimization/Minorize-Maximization (MM) objective function optimization pattern. clusterNOR introduces semi-external memory I/O optimizations and cache friendly NUMA scheduling policies for both hierarchical and non-hierarchical clustering algorithms. We demonstrate how these optimizations lead to performance improvements of up to an order magnitude over state-of-the-art clustering frameworks.
Bio:
Disa Mhembere is a Ph.D. candidate in computer science at the Johns Hopkins University. He received both a masters in engineering management in 2013 and a masters in computer science in 2015 from Johns Hopkins University. During his Ph.D. he interned with IBM Research and Kyndi Inc. Disa was awarded the Paul V. Renoff Computer Science Graduate Fellowship in 2014, the UPE Special Recognition award in 2014 and the UPE Academic Achievement Award in 2017. He also received the best presentation award at the High-Performance Parallel and Distributed Computing (HPDC) Conference in 2017.
Website:
https://cs.jhu.edu/~disa

Host:
Randal Burns

Reatha Holt
Sr. Administrative Coordinator
Johns Hopkins University
Computer Science Department
Malone 160
410-516-8775

.F Reatha Holt <rholt6@jhu.edu>
.S Student Defense: Disa Mhembere, Johns Hopkins University - April 29 ,2019-11:00 PM
.D Thu, 25 Apr 2019 20:18:14 +0000
.M
Ph.D. Candidate: Disa Mhembere

Data & Time: Monday, April 29, 2019 at 11:00 AM - 1:00 PM

Location: Malone 228

Title: Scalable Graph Analysis and Clustering on Commodity Hardware

Abstract:
Today's large-scale datasets necessitate scalable data analysis frameworks and libraries. Traditional distributed memory solutions neglect optimizations for prevalent Non-Uniform Memory Access (NUMA) architectures. Additionally, distributed memory solutions often solely rely on process-level parallelism and thus forgo shared and external memory optimizations, leading to suboptimal overall performance. This thesis explores the effects of NUMA-awareness and fine-grain I/O optimizations from SSDs to improve hardware minimality, scalability and memory parallelism in graph analytics and community detection. Our computation optimizations target data that reside either (i) in-memory, (ii) semi-externally, or (iii) in distributed memory. We first present Graphyti, a semi-external memory graph library built on the FlashGraph framework to demonstrate key design principles for vertex-centric, semi-external memory (SEM) graph applications. Graphyti on a single thick node achieves comparable performance to popular distributed graph libraries in a cluster. We then address web-scale community detection and present the clusterNOR framework. We advance the state-of-the-art for memory parallel NUMA computation of k-means and subsequently clustering algorithms that follow the Majorize-Minimization/Minorize-Maximization (MM) objective function optimization pattern. clusterNOR introduces semi-external memory I/O optimizations and cache friendly NUMA scheduling policies for both hierarchical and non-hierarchical clustering algorithms. We demonstrate how these optimizations lead to performance improvements of up to an order magnitude over state-of-the-art clustering frameworks.
Bio:
Disa Mhembere is a Ph.D. candidate in computer science at the Johns Hopkins University. He received both a masters in engineering management in 2013 and a masters in computer science in 2015 from Johns Hopkins University. During his Ph.D. he interned with IBM Research and Kyndi Inc. Disa was awarded the Paul V. Renoff Computer Science Graduate Fellowship in 2014, the UPE Special Recognition award in 2014 and the UPE Academic Achievement Award in 2017. He also received the best presentation award at the High-Performance Parallel and Distributed Computing (HPDC) Conference in 2017.
Website:
https://cs.jhu.edu/~disa

Host:
Randal Burns

Reatha Holt
Sr. Administrative Coordinator
Johns Hopkins University
Computer Science Department
Malone 160
410-516-8775

.I 38
.L 4
.F Reatha Holt <rholt6@jhu.edu>
.S Student Defense: James Browne, Johns Hopkins University - April 25 ,2019-10:30 AM *** Malone 228 New Location ***
.D Thu, 25 Apr 2019 12:01:33 +0000
.M
Ph.D. Candidate: James Browne

Data & Time: Thursday, April 25, 2019 at 1030 AM - 12:30 PM

Location: ***Malone 228- New Location***

Title: Oblique Sparse Projection Forest Optimization: A Faster Randomer Forest


Abstract:
The proliferation of scientific and industrial sensors is causing an accelerating deluge of data the processing of which into actionable knowledge requires fast and accurate machine learning methods. A class of algorithms suited to process these large amounts of data is decision forests, widely used methods known for their versatility, state of the art inference, and fast model training.
Oblique Sparse Projection Forests - OSPFs - are a subset of decision forests, which provide data inference superior to other methods.  Despite providing state of the art inference and having a computational complexity similar to other popular decision forests, there are no SPF implementations that scale beyond trivially sized datasets.

We  explore  whether  OSPF  training  and  inference  speeds  can  compete with other popular decision forest variants despite an algorithmic incompatibility which prevent OSPFs from using traditional forest training optimizations. First, using R, we implement a highly extensible proof of concept version of a recently conceived OSPF, Randomer Forest, shown to provide state
of the art results on many datasets and provide this system for general use via CRAN. We then develop and implement a postprocessing method, Forest Packing, to pack the nodes of a trained forest into a novel data structure and modify the ensemble traversal method to accelerate forest based inferences. Finally, we develop FastRerF, an optimized version of Randomer Forest which dynamically performs forest packing during training.

The initial implementation in R provided training speeds in line with other decision forest systems and scaled better with additional resources, but used an excessive amount of memory and provided slow inference speeds.  The development  of  Forest  Packing  increased  inference  throughput  by  almost an order of magnitude as compared to other systems while greatly reducing prediction latency.  FastRerF model training is faster than other popular decision forest systems when using similar parameters and trains Random Forests faster than the current state of the art.  Overall, we provide data scientists a novel OSPF system with R and Python front ends, which trains and
predicts faster than other decision forest implementations.


Bio:
James Browne received a Bachelor's degree in Computer Science from the United States Military Academy at West Point in 2002. In 2012 he received a dual Masters of Science Degree in Computer Science and Applied Mathematics from the Naval Postgraduate School in Monterey California where he received the Rear Admiral Grace Murray Hopper Computer Science Award for excellence in computer science research.  He enrolled in the Computer Science Ph.D. program at Johns Hopkins University in 2016 and, after graduation, will become an instructor in the Electrical Engineering and Computer Science Department at the United States Military Academy.

Host:
Randal Burns

Reatha Holt
Sr. Administrative Coordinator
Johns Hopkins University
Computer Science Department
Malone 160
410-516-8775

.F President Daniels <jhucommunications@jhu.edu>
.D Wed, 24 Apr 2019 14:52:06 +0000
.M
Is this email not displaying properly? View it in your browser (https://mailchi.mp/b7afd662478f/10x2020-progress-report-254097?e=278d787a13) .

44fee69e-ac7f-455d-8af8-a22b870314a0.jpg

Dear Faculty, Students, and Staff:
In 2013, we laid out an ambitious set of goals for the university to achieve by the end of the decade—the Ten by Twenty. With input from and consultation with members of the JHU community, we pushed ourselves to aim high.
As we approach 2020, we are not only reaching even further but redefining what’s possible for us to accomplish together. In this, our third Ten by Twenty progress report (https://10x2020progress.jhu.edu/2019/?utm_source=JHU+Broadcast+Messages+-+Synced+List&utm_campaign=91cc48dc91-EMAIL_CAMPAIGN_2019_02_25_06_47_COPY_01&utm_medium=email&utm_term=0_9cf4956bf9-91cc48dc91-69746085&mc_cid=91cc48dc91&mc_eid=[UNIQID]) , we mark our successes through 2018 and share the many ways in which our university is striving to advance core priorities.
Since our last progress report, we have broken records for faculty hiring and research support, pushed the boundaries of space exploration, and increased access to a Hopkins education thanks to extraordinary support for financial aid that now places Johns Hopkins in the pantheon of great universities that are permanently need-blind and no-loan. Our momentum is also building with the launch of interdisciplinary centers for learning and health care, and new tools for student, faculty, and staff success.
We are humbled by all that the Hopkins community has accomplished since the start of the Ten by Twenty, and excited about what’s in store in its final two years as we make new investments in sustainability, a long-awaited student center, and a new home for Hopkins programs in the heart of the nation’s capital.
I invite you to explore this report and share your thoughts on ways we can deepen and extend our impact by 2020 and beyond.

Sincerely,
Ronald J. Daniels


#https://10x2020progress.jhu.edu/2019/ten-by-twenty-videos/

When: March 28 - May 1, 12am
Where: MSE Library, Homewood Campus
Summary: This traveling multimedia exhibition by the German Federal Ministry of Justice and Consumer Protection explores the Nazi infiltration of the post-1945 West German justice ministry and the legal and political consequences for the young Federal Republic.

.L 4
.F Reatha Holt <rholt6@jhu.edu>
.S Student Defense: James Browne, Johns Hopkins University - April 25 ,2019-10:30 AM Malone 107
.D Tue, 23 Apr 2019 12:00:31 +0000
.M
Ph.D. Candidate: James Browne

Data & Time: Thursday, April 25, 2019 at 1030 AM - 12:30 PM

Location: Malone 107

Title: Oblique Sparse Projection Forest Optimization: A Faster Randomer Forest


Abstract:
The proliferation of scientific and industrial sensors is causing an accelerating deluge of data the processing of which into actionable knowledge requires fast and accurate machine learning methods. A class of algorithms suited to process these large amounts of data is decision forests, widely used methods known for their versatility, state of the art inference, and fast model training.
Oblique Sparse Projection Forests - OSPFs - are a subset of decision forests, which provide data inference superior to other methods.  Despite providing state of the art inference and having a computational complexity similar to other popular decision forests, there are no SPF implementations that scale beyond trivially sized datasets.

We  explore  whether  OSPF  training  and  inference  speeds  can  compete with other popular decision forest variants despite an algorithmic incompatibility which prevent OSPFs from using traditional forest training optimizations. First, using R, we implement a highly extensible proof of concept version of a recently conceived OSPF, Randomer Forest, shown to provide state
of the art results on many datasets and provide this system for general use via CRAN. We then develop and implement a postprocessing method, Forest Packing, to pack the nodes of a trained forest into a novel data structure and modify the ensemble traversal method to accelerate forest based inferences. Finally, we develop FastRerF, an optimized version of Randomer Forest which dynamically performs forest packing during training.

The initial implementation in R provided training speeds in line with other decision forest systems and scaled better with additional resources, but used an excessive amount of memory and provided slow inference speeds.  The development  of  Forest  Packing  increased  inference  throughput  by  almost an order of magnitude as compared to other systems while greatly reducing prediction latency.  FastRerF model training is faster than other popular decision forest systems when using similar parameters and trains Random Forests faster than the current state of the art.  Overall, we provide data scientists a novel OSPF system with R and Python front ends, which trains and
predicts faster than other decision forest implementations.


Bio:
James Browne received a Bachelor's degree in Computer Science from the United States Military Academy at West Point in 2002. In 2012 he received a dual Masters of Science Degree in Computer Science and Applied Mathematics from the Naval Postgraduate School in Monterey California where he received the Rear Admiral Grace Murray Hopper Computer Science Award for excellence in computer science research.  He enrolled in the Computer Science Ph.D. program at Johns Hopkins University in 2016 and, after graduation, will become an instructor in the Electrical Engineering and Computer Science Department at the United States Military Academy.

Host:
Randal Burns

Reatha Holt
Sr. Administrative Coordinator
Johns Hopkins University
Computer Science Department
Malone 160
410-516-8775

.F "Maple Medical, LLP" <patientoutreach@maplemedical.com>
.S Measles Immunizations
.M
Having trouble viewing this email?

http://campaign.r20.constantcontact.com/render?m=1104865670770&ca=d17c39ca-ec7b-462c-bc49-09ddcf5c3471

~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~

Dear Patient,
Many of you have expressed concern about the recent measles epidemic and your risk
of contracting the disease.  If you know as a fact that you have previously had 
measles, then you are immune.  If you are uncertain, our recommendation at this 
time is that you obtain an MMR blood test to test for immunity to the measles virus.
 If the blood test shows low immunity, then you should receive one booster of MMR
vaccine.  Please see below for the CDC published MMR vaccine side effects.
PLEASE BE AWARE THAT BOTH THE BLOOD TEST AND THE VACCINE MAY NOT BE COVERED BY INSURANCE.
As always, your health and the heath of your loved ones is our primary concern.
Sincerely,
The Doctors and Staff at Maple Medical
~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~

MMR Vaccine Side Effects
The MMR vaccine is very safe, and it is effective at preventing measles, mumps, 
and rubella. Vaccines, like any medicine, can have side effects. Most people who
 get MMR vaccine do not have any serious problems with it. Getting MMR vaccine is
much safer than getting measles, mumps or rubella.
Common Side Effects of MMR Vaccine:

* Sore arm from the shot
* Fever
* Mild rash
* Temporary pain and stiffness in the joints, mostly in teenage or adult women who
did not already have immunity to the rubella component of the vaccine
MMR vaccine has been linked with a very small risk of febrile seizures (seizures
 or jerking caused by fever). Febrile seizures following MMR are rare and are not
associated with any long-term effects. Because the risk of febrile seizures increases
as infants get older, it is recommended that they get vaccinated as soon as recommended.
Some people may experience swelling in the cheeks or neck. MMR vaccine rarely causes
a temporary low platelet count, which can cause a bleeding disorder that usually
 goes away without treatment and is not life threatening.
Extremely rarely, a person may have a serious allergic reaction to MMR vaccine. 
Anyone who has ever had a life-threatening allergic reaction to the antibiotic neomycin,
or any other component of MMR vaccine, should not get the vaccine.
Source:
https://www.cdc.gov/vaccinesafety/vaccines/mmr-vaccine.html?CDC_AA_refVal=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.cdc.gov%2Fvaccinesafety%2Fvaccines%2Fmmr%2Findex.html#side-effects
[https://www.cdc.gov/vaccinesafety/vaccines/mmr-vaccine.html?CDC_AA_refVal=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.cdc.gov%2Fvaccinesafety%2Fvaccines%2Fmmr%2Findex.html#side-effects]
~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~

.I 55
.L 4
.F Reatha Holt <rholt6@jhu.edu>
.S CS Seminar Speaker: Ke Li – "Nearest Neighbour Search and Generative Modelling"
.D Wed, 17 Apr 2019 19:39:21 +0000
.M
Speaker:  Ke Li, UC Berkeley
Date & Time: April 18, 2019 at 10:30 am
Location: Hackerman B17
Title: Nearest Neighbour Search and Generative Modelling

Abstract:

Machine learning is subject to the limits of computation, and advances in algorithms can open up new possibilities for machine learning. The problem of nearest neighbour search arises commonly in machine learning; unfortunately, despite over 40 years of research, prior sublinear algorithms for exact nearest neighbour search suffer from the curse of dimensionality, that is, an exponential dependence of query time complexity on either the ambient or the intrinsic dimensionality. In the first part of this talk, I will present Dynamic Continuous Indexing (DCI), a new family of exact randomized algorithms that avoids exponential dependence on both the ambient and the intrinsic dimensionality. This advance enables us to develop a new method for generative modelling, known as Implicit Maximum Likelihood Estimation (IMLE), which I will present in the second part of the talk. IMLE can be shown to be equivalent to maximum likelihood under some conditions and simultaneously overcomes three fundamental issues of generative adversarial nets (GANs), namely mode collapse, vanishing gradients and training instability. I will illustrate why mode collapse happens in GANs and how IMLE overcomes it, and also demonstrate empirical results on image synthesis. I will close off with a brief discussion of another approach I introduced, known as Learning to Optimize.

Bio:

Ke Li is a Ph.D. candidate at UC Berkeley advised by Prof. Jitendra Malik. He is interested in a broad range of topics in machine learning, and also enjoys working on computer vision, natural language processing and algorithms. He is particularly passionate about tackling long-standing fundamental problems that cannot be tackled with a straightforward application of conventional techniques. He received his Hon. B.Sc. in Computer Science from the University of Toronto and is grateful for the support of the Natural Sciences and Engineering Research Council of Canada (NSERC).


Reatha Holt
Sr. Administrative Coordinator
Johns Hopkins University
Computer Science Department
Malone 160
410-516-8775

.I 59
.L 4
.F Zachary Burwell <zburwel1@jhu.edu>
.S FW: Summer research projects
.D Tue, 16 Apr 2019 14:25:42 +0000
.M
FYI if you're interested in a summer research opportunity!

========================================================================================================================
Summer research project opportunities

Our group at the Malone Center for Engineering in Healthcare is working on projects that utilize computer science areas like machine learning, crowdsourcing and virtual reality simulation to improve surgery training and assessment. Our projects are multidisciplinary in nature and include clinical and education collaborators in addition to computer science and engineering investigators. There are a lot of projects underway in the space of colonoscopy procedures, cataract surgery, head and neck surgery, robot-assisted surgery, and simulation-based training using virtual reality. Finally, these projects have various components that can range from sophisticated computer programming needs to very low level programming knowledge. Please write an email to both of us Anand Malpani (anandmalpani@jhu.edu<mailto:anandmalpani@jhu.edu>) and Swaroop Vedula (swaroop@jhu.edu<mailto:swaroop@jhu.edu>) with the following details:

  1.  Resume
  2.  Relevant courses and projects if any
  3.  1-2 sentences indicating why you are interested in this work
  4.  Availability during Summer 2019 - vacation, other projects and commitments
  5.  Would you want to continue the project into Fall
  6.  If you are a masters student, are you interested in pursuing a thesis project - this would be with Prof. Gregory Hager provided he approves

Note: these are not necessarily paid positions.

Thanks,
Anand

.I 61
.L 4
.F Reatha Holt <rholt6@jhu.edu>
.S Gerald M. Masson Distinguished Lecture Series Speaker Schedule: Avrim Blum, April 16, 2019
.D Tue, 16 Apr 2019 12:00:55 +0000
.M
Speaker:  Avrim Blum, Toyota Technological Institute at Chicago
Date & Time: April 16,2019 at 10:30 am
Location: Hackerman B17
Title:  "Algorithmic fairness in Online Decision-making"
Abstract:
There is growing concern about fairness in algorithmic decision making: Are algorithmic decisions treating different groups fairly? How can we make them fairer? What do we even mean by fair? In this talk I will discuss some of our work on this topic, focusing on the setting of online decision making. For instance, a classic result states that given a collection of predictors, one can adaptively combine them to perform nearly as well as the best of those predictors in hindsight (achieve "no regret") without any stochastic assumptions. Can one extend this guarantee so that if the predictors are themselves fair (according to a given definition), then the overall combination is fair as well (according to the same definition)? I will discuss this and other issues. This is joint work with Suriya Gunasekar, Thodoris Lykouris, and Nati Srebro.
Bio:
Professor Blum's main research interests are in Theoretical Computer Science and Machine Learning, including Machine Learning Theory, Approximation Algorithms, Algorithmic Game Theory, and Database Privacy, as well as connections among them. Some current specific interests include multi-agent learning, multi-task learning, semi-supervised learning, and the design of incentive systems. He is also known for his past work in Al Planning. Prof. Blum has served as Program Chair for the IEE Symposium and Foundations of Computer Science (FOCS) and the Conference on Learing Theory (COLT). He has served as Chair of the ACM SIGACT Committee for the Advancement of Theoretical Computer Science and on the SIGACT Executive Committee.

Host:

Raman Arora

Reatha Holt
Sr. Administrative Coordinator
Johns Hopkins University
Computer Science Department
Malone 160
410-516-8775

.L 4
.F Reatha Holt <rholt6@jhu.edu>
.S Gerald M. Masson Distinguished Lecture Series Speaker Schedule: Avrim Blum, April 16, 2019
.D Mon, 15 Apr 2019 13:29:37 +0000
.M
Speaker:  Avrim Blum, Toyota Technological Institute at Chicago
Date & Time: April 16,2019 at 10:30 am
Location: Hackerman B17
Title:  "Algorithmic fairness in Online Decision-making"
Abstract:
There is growing concern about fairness in algorithmic decision making: Are algorithmic decisions treating different groups fairly? How can we make them fairer? What do we even mean by fair? In this talk I will discuss some of our work on this topic, focusing on the setting of online decision making. For instance, a classic result states that given a collection of predictors, one can adaptively combine them to perform nearly as well as the best of those predictors in hindsight (achieve "no regret") without any stochastic assumptions. Can one extend this guarantee so that if the predictors are themselves fair (according to a given definition), then the overall combination is fair as well (according to the same definition)? I will discuss this and other issues. This is joint work with Suriya Gunasekar, Thodoris Lykouris, and Nati Srebro.
Bio:
Professor Blum's main research interests are in Theoretical Computer Science and Machine Learning, including Machine Learning Theory, Approximation Algorithms, Algorithmic Game Theory, and Database Privacy, as well as connections among them. Some current specific interests include multi-agent learning, multi-task learning, semi-supervised learning, and the design of incentive systems. He is also known for his past work in Al Planning. Prof. Blum has served as Program Chair for the IEE Symposium and Foundations of Computer Science (FOCS) and the Conference on Learing Theory (COLT). He has served as Chair of the ACM SIGACT Committee for the Advancement of Theoretical Computer Science and on the SIGACT Executive Committee.

Host:

Raman Arora

Reatha Holt
Sr. Administrative Coordinator
Johns Hopkins University
Computer Science Department
Malone 160
410-516-8775

.S Roadmap on Diversity and Inclusion Progress Report
.F President Daniels and Provost Kumar <jhucommunications@johnshopkins.edu>
.D Fri, 12 Apr 2019 19:29:49 +0000
.M
Is this email not displaying correctly? View it in your browser (https://mailchi.mp/1526439f1f3b/roadmap-on-diversity-and-inclusion-progress-report?e=278d787a13) .
http://www.jhu.edu?utm_source=JHU+Broadcast+Messages+-+Synced+List&utm_campaign=be566bdcc4-EMAIL_CAMPAIGN_2019_04_09_07_26&utm_medium=email&utm_term=0_9cf4956bf9-be566bdcc4-69746085&mc_cid=be566bdcc4&mc_eid=[UNIQID]

Dear Faculty, Students, and Staff:

Over the past several months, we have shared updates with the university community highlighting the composition of our faculty, the work of our Office of Institutional Equity, and the promising results of our three-year economic inclusion program, HopkinsLocal. These vital initiatives, taking place across the university and in partnership with leaders and neighbors across Baltimore City, reflect Johns Hopkins’ sustained commitment to engaging fully a diversity of people, backgrounds, experiences, and thought. Such diversity is fundamental to our mission and to the university’s continued excellence.

Today, we release our second progress report on the JHU Roadmap on Diversity and Inclusion (https://diversity.jhu.edu/wp-content/uploads/2019/04/Roadmap-Progress-Report-2019.pdf?utm_source=JHU+Broadcast+Messages+-+Synced+List&utm_campaign=be566bdcc4-EMAIL_CAMPAIGN_2019_04_09_07_26&utm_medium=email&utm_term=0_9cf4956bf9-be566bdcc4-69746085&mc_cid=be566bdcc4&mc_eid=[UNIQID]) , with current information on the broad array of work and actions that are underway to advance diversity and inclusion across our campuses. This update also supports the promise we made when we launched the Roadmap in 2016: to be transparent and accountable as we work toward our ambitious goals.

Among the highlights of the report:
* The extraordinary $1.8 billion gift from Michael Bloomberg to support undergraduate financial aid had an immediate impact after its announcement last fall, allowing us to launch and accelerate efforts to recruit diverse students from every socioeconomic background and to ensure they have access to comprehensive support and opportunities when they arrive on campus.

* We published our second Faculty Composition report, detailing the racial, ethnic, and gender composition of our faculty to the departmental level. The report differentiates between underrepresented minority (URM) faculty, who identify as part of racial or ethnic groups that have traditionally been underrepresented within higher education, including black or African-American, Hispanic, American Indian, or Hawaiian or other Pacific Islander, and minority faculty, which encompasses the URM categories plus individuals identifying as Asian.
The report, based on November 2017 data, provides key snapshots and comparisons against our own first progress report and peer institutions. We are encouraged that our overall faculty growth rate (5%) was surpassed by our growth rate in female professorial faculty (13%), minority professorial faculty (11%), and URM professorial faculty (18%). And while there is still significant work ahead, we are pleased to see universitywide increases in female faculty (from 42% to 44%), in all minority faculty (from 30% to 32%), and in URM faculty (from 8% to 9%).

* Later this spring, we will be publishing our first Staff Composition Report, which is similar to our faculty (https://provost.jhu.edu/wp-content/uploads/sites/4/2019/03/Report-on-Faculty-Composition-2019.pdf?utm_source=JHU+Broadcast+Messages+-+Synced+List&utm_campaign=be566bdcc4-EMAIL_CAMPAIGN_2019_04_09_07_26&utm_medium=email&utm_term=0_9cf4956bf9-be566bdcc4-69746085&mc_cid=be566bdcc4&mc_eid=[UNIQID]) and graduate student (http://web.jhu.edu/administration/provost/reports_resources/JHU%20Report%20on%20Graduate%20School%20Diversity.pdf//?utm_source=JHU+Broadcast+Messages+-+Synced+List&utm_campaign=be566bdcc4-EMAIL_CAMPAIGN_2019_04_09_07_26&utm_medium=email&utm_term=0_9cf4956bf9-be566bdcc4-69746085&mc_cid=be566bdcc4&mc_eid=[UNIQID]) composition reports and underscores an enhanced focus on staff diversity.

* The Faculty Diversity Initiative (FDI), now in its fourth year, continues to support our efforts to recruit diverse faculty and to build out the systems and processes needed to retain and bolster the development of all faculty. Between 2016 (the first year of the FDI) and 2018, the percentage of new faculty hires who identified as underrepresented minorities (URM) was relatively consistent (10.5% and 10.8%, respectively), while the rate of departures for URM faculty fell by nearly half (from 9.5% to 4.9%).

* We expanded health benefits for students, providing many with lower deductibles for health care, new options for dental and vision coverage, and improved mental health coverage. We broadened the university’s child care benefit to include residents, interns, and full-time doctoral students or postdocs. And for employees, we further expanded family-support benefits and the Employee Assistance Program, and have focused on enhanced outreach to increase the understanding and use of available benefits.

* We are updating the university’s foundational diversity statements and policies—specifically, Johns Hopkins’ Diversity, Inclusion, and Equity Statement, and a new Discrimination and Harassment Policy and Procedures—to reflect a contemporary and shared understanding of our community’s values and expectations. Both will be released this spring.

* We published our first annual report of the Office of Institutional Equity in fall 2018, providing background and specific data on our response to reports of sexual misconduct and other forms of discrimination and harassment.

* We met our three-year goals for the HopkinsLocal economic inclusion program, committing 23.5 percent of our addressable construction spending to minority-owned, women-owned, and disadvantaged businesses; hiring 1,017 people from distressed city ZIP codes into targeted positions; and increasing our spending with local suppliers by $54.3 million. We also committed to launching the second round of this important program this summer.

Since the adoption of the JHU Roadmap on Diversity and Inclusion in 2016, the commitment of our students, staff, faculty, and divisional leadership to this effort—and the continued focus of our board of trustees—has allowed us to make significant progress in a number of key areas, from faculty recruitment and retention to local economic inclusion. Our work, however, is far from complete and our commitments are not static. Over the past year alone, our community has engaged passionately around issues such as safety and security, disability services, and federal immigration policy—all of which implicate principles at the core of the Roadmap. We must continue to embrace these debates and ensure our values drive our responses to these and many other important concerns.

We look forward to the work ahead and hope you will read the report and continue to share your feedback and ideas through the diversity.jhu.eduwebsite or by emailing diversityroadmap@jhu.edu (mailto:diversityroadmap@jhu.edu) .

Sincerely,

Ronald J. Daniels
President

Sunil Kumar
Provost and Senior Vice President for Academic Affairs

.I 74
.L 4
.F Reatha Holt <rholt6@jhu.edu>
.S FW: Gerald M. Masson Distinguished Lecture Series Speaker Schedule: Avrim Blum, April 16, 2019
.D Thu, 11 Apr 2019 11:47:31 +0000
.M
Speaker:  Avrim Blum, Toyota Technological Institute at Chicago
Date & Time: April 16,2019 at 10:30 am
Location: Hackerman B17
Title:  "Algorithmic fairness in Online Decision-making"
Abstract:
There is growing concern about fairness in algorithmic decision making: Are algorithmic decisions treating different groups fairly? How can we make them fairer? What do we even mean by fair? In this talk I will discuss some of our work on this topic, focusing on the setting of online decision making. For instance, a classic result states that given a collection of predictors, one can adaptively combine them to perform nearly as well as the best of those predictors in hindsight (achieve "no regret") without any stochastic assumptions. Can one extend this guarantee so that if the predictors are themselves fair (according to a given definition), then the overall combination is fair as well (according to the same definition)? I will discuss this and other issues. This is joint work with Suriya Gunasekar, Thodoris Lykouris, and Nati Srebro.
Bio:
Professor Blum's main research interests are in Theoretical Computer Science and Machine Learning, including Machine Learning Theory, Approximation Algorithms, Algorithmic Game Theory, and Database Privacy, as well as connections among them. Some current specific interests include multi-agent learning, multi-task learning, semi-supervised learning, and the design of incentive systems. He is also known for his past work in Al Planning. Prof. Blum has served as Program Chair for the IEE Symposium and Foundations of Computer Science (FOCS) and the Conference on Learing Theory (COLT). He has served as Chair of the ACM SIGACT Committee for the Advancement of Theoretical Computer Science and on the SIGACT Executive Committee.

Host:

Raman Arora

Reatha Holt
Sr. Administrative Coordinator
Johns Hopkins University
Computer Science Department
Malone 160
410-516-8775

.S FW: Summer Data Science Fellowship Opportunity - UK
.D Tue, 9 Apr 2019 20:51:55 +0000
.M
From: Michael Li <michael.li@thedataincubator.org>
Sent: Tuesday, April 9, 2019 3:31 PM
To: contactus@cs.jhu.edu
Subject: Summer Data Science Fellowship Opportunity - UK


Hi,

We wanted to remind you that our summer application deadline is only a few weeks away! We would appreciate your help forwarding this opportunity on to interested Masters or PhD students who are looking for a Fellowship opportunity this summer. This is an especially great opportunity for those students graduating and job searching in May.

________________________________

Program: The Data Incubator is an intensive 8 week fellowship that prepares masters students, PhDs, and postdocs in STEM and social science fields seeking industry careers as data scientists. The program is free for Fellows and supported by sponsorships from hundreds of employers across multiple industries. In response to the overwhelming interest in our earlier sessions, we will be holding another fellowship.

Who Should Apply: Anyone who has already obtained a masters or PhD degree or who is within one year of graduating with a masters or PhD is welcome to apply. Applications from international students are welcome. Everyone else is encouraged to sign-up for a future session<http://sglt.thedataincubator.org/wf/click?upn=GkQRC-2FpDle1-2FgK2pc9Y4OvrxyUW9eQTElbxUoSCKuS4j0eUu5X7ZkagNKrNRAWAiEiz65BkvBu3M8aQIeXKw4eLYoe2eulGqBwY0BEsjZ0w2-2FpeuEQEUEF1M8cwnMpBB_hfIqhjxrH5PXl2rHT1sLDaYs-2FbyCXKl57OtGh0IGeQSqpQmLmbHmQKYVpAsbQ-2BKehNVGJpyw3976-2FJf3FAMzWXj8PHQd2tlqoaHQ1ZJOm3WLW2DOZZcYjzpZAQJfBXHYJHSgHsLlRbzF88GCevInu3XO3N35ti9USmJ8-2FXsZLiP9hLuoaTERwQHRCg5ZeMtNMA58GKO1BIUMuXoHQwDO-2BGkRd9iUgQ4OdOXhF7fQVMYDeAb71C-2F6jT1ZiAITu-2F9ftNZWLFSHpw93R6rdBZyW-2Bw-3D-3D>.

.S Invitation to lecture from Nobel Laureate in Physics on April 30
.F Larry Nagahara and John Toscano <wsecommunications@jhu.edu>
.D Tue, 9 Apr 2019 14:27:10 +0000
.M
Eric Cornell to present "Looking for Fossils of the Big Bang" at Morgan State University.

From the Office of the Dean
View this email in your browser (https://mailchi.mp/jhu/hnpc-lecture-eric-cornell?e=a57d9a1452)
From the Office of the Dean
Dear faculty and students,

You are cordially invited to attend a lecture (https://www.morgan.edu/school_of_computer_mathematical_and_natural_sciences/invited_lecture_dr_eric_cornell_nobel_laureate_in_physics.html) by Dr. Eric Cornell, a 2001 Nobel Prize Laureate for Physics, to be held at Morgan State University. Cornell, a physicist with NIST, will be giving a lecture titled, “Looking for Fossils of the Big Bang,” Tuesday, April 30, 2019 from 10 to 11:30 a.m. in the New Student Center, Theatre Room 133. The Johns Hopkins NIST PREP (Professional Research Experience Program) Consortium will provide transportation to Morgan State for those who need it. Please RSVP by Wednesday, April 17, 2019 by emailing Michelle Cagan (mailto:mcagan2@jhu.edu) .

The Hopkins NIST PREP Consortium, or HNPC, was designed to bring faculty and students of all levels new opportunities to work with NIST scientists and gain real world experiences in understanding industry demands and advancing measurement science and technology. The consortium is led by JHU, with Morgan State University and State University of New York at Binghamton as participating members. All opportunities are open to students of all levels, research faculty, and graduates with bachelor’s degrees.
* What: “Looking for Fossils of the Big Bang,” (https://www.morgan.edu/school_of_computer_mathematical_and_natural_sciences/invited_lecture_dr_eric_cornell_nobel_laureate_in_physics.html) Dr. Eric Cornell, Nobel Laureate in Physics
* When: Tuesday, April 30, 2019, 10 to 11:30 a.m.
* Where: Morgan State University, New Student Center, Theatre Room 133
* RSVP by Wednesday, April 17 to Michelle Cagan at mcagan2@jhu.edu (mailto:mcagan2@jhu.edu) and indicate if you would also like transportation

We hope you will join us for this special event at Morgan State University. Please remember to RSVP by April 17 to Michelle Cagan at mcagan2@jhu.edu (mailto:mcagan2@jhu.edu) , and also indicate if you will need transportation to and from the event.

Sincerely,
Larry Nagahara
Associate Dean of Research
Whiting School of Engineering
John Toscano
Vice Dean for Natural Sciences
Krieger School of Arts and Sciences

Where: MSE Library, Homewood Campus

     We also strongly recommend enabling two-step verification. You can learn more about it or get help setting it up here: https://help.groupme.com/hc/articles/360000579167-GroupMe-two-step-verification-FAQ

Arts+Culture

Movements: Black Print Culture in the United States, 1773–1940 (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/02/11/black-print-culture/)
When: Feb 11 - May 24, 12am
Where: MSE Library, Homewood Campus
Summary: An exhibition exploring the circulation and contribution of black print culture through original items drawn from the Sheridan Libraries Special Collections.

The Rosenburg—The Federal Ministry of Justice in the Shadow of the Nazi Past (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/03/28/the-rosenburgthe-federal-ministry-of-justice-in-the-shadow-of-the-nazi-past/)
When: March 28 - May 1, 12am
Where: MSE Library, Homewood Campus
Summary: This traveling multimedia exhibition by the German Federal Ministry of Justice and Consumer Protection explores the Nazi infiltration of the post-1945 West German justice ministry and the legal and political consequences for the young Federal Republic.

Reading: Because You're My Mother (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/05/reading-of-the-original-play/)
When: Today, 10am-12:15pm
Where: Columbia Center
Summary: Featuring local talent from The Capitol Steps, Toby's Dinner Theater, Howard Community College's Rep Stage, and Silhouette Stages, this two-act play reading of Paula Stone's Because You're My Mother is sponsored by the Osher Lifelong Learning Institute.

Student Life

DREAMS: A Celebration of Undergraduate Research, Creative, and Scholarly Projects (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/05/dreams-a-celebration-of-undergraduate-research-creative-and-scholarly-projects/)
When: Today, 1-4pm
Where: O'Connor Rec Center, Homewood Campus
Summary: DREAMS is the Hopkins Office for Undergraduate Research's annual celebration of the research, scholarly, and creative projects of Johns Hopkins undergraduate students. All undergraduates interested in presenting must register in advance.

Express your stress workshop (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/05/express-your-stress-workshop-0/)
When: Today, 5-7pm
Where: Ames Hall, Homewood Campus
Summary: Express Your Stress is a free workshop created to give Hopkins students a fun opportunity to relieve stress and learn how to use art to improve their well-being. Peggy Kolodny, a professional art therapist will lead students through activities including making a therapeutic collage and experimenting with watercolor paint and oil pastels. Students will leave this workshop with ideas on how to use art to relieve stress in their daily lives. Art beginners are welcome. This event is sponsored by Art Gives and the Counseling Center.

Express your stress workshop (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/05/express-your-stress-workshop/)
When: Today, 5-7pm
Where: Ames Hall, Homewood Campus

Peggy Kolodny, a professional art therapist will lead students through activities including making a therapeutic collage and experimenting with watercolor paint and oil pastels. Students will leave this workshop with ideas on how to use art to relieve stress in their daily lives. Art beginners welcome. This is sponsored by the Counseling Center.

Johns Hopkins University Muslim Association Spring Banquet 2019 (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/05/jhuma-spring-banquet-2019/)
When: Today, 6-9pm
Where: Levering Hall, Homewood Campus
Summary: The Johns Hopkins University Muslim Association's annual Spring Banquet is their most popular event. This year, the theme is "Our Best Selves" and the event supports The Family Tree, a Baltimore organization that helps prevent child abuse while empowering families and building communities.

Where: Charles Commons, Homewood Campus
Summary: Please join the Jonathan A. Jarzyna Memorial No Limit Texas Hold'em Tournament during the Johns Hopkins Alumni Weekend, honoring Johns Hopkins alumnus Jon Jarzyna, KSAS '97, sponsored by fellow alumnus Matt Zaft, KSAS '97. The event includes refreshments and prizes. Proceeds benefit the MSPCA.

Consent in Queer Sex Work Spaces (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/05/consent-in-queer-sex-work-spaces/)
When: Today, 7-8pm
Where: Gilman Hall, Homewood Campus
Summary: Domina Vontana, a professional dominatrix from Washington, D.C., will speak about the intersections of queer history and identity with BDSM as well as navigating consent and safety in the world of professional BDSM.

Women in STEM Symposium 2019 (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/06/women-in-stem-symposium-2019/)
When: Saturday, 8am-5pm
Where: Turner Concourse, East Baltimore Campus
Summary: Women of Whiting is excited to host a one-day symposium filled with interactive workshops, poster sessions, and networking opportunities.

Acura @ Homecoming (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/06/acura-homecoming/)
When: Saturday, 9am-4pm
Where: Homewood Campus
Summary: Jay Way Media invites students to their table at Homecoming to learn more about the 2019 Acura ILX, see the car in person, and get giveaway items, including free T-shirts.

Hop Talks: An Alumni Panel Discussion featuring Dr. Zeid Ra'ad (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/06/hop-talks-an-alumni-panel-discussion-featuring-dr-zeid-raad-former-united-nations-high-commission/)
When: Saturday, 10:30am-12pm
Where: Hodson Hall, Homewood Campus
Summary: Dr. Steven David, a professor of Political Science, will moderate a thoughtful conversation of alumni panelists, including alumnus Dr. Zeid Ra'ad (Class of 1987) who served on the United Nations High Commissioner for Human Rights. The panel will discuss human rights, the role of the UN, and the state of the world.

LGBTQ History Walking Tour of Charles Village (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/06/lgbtq-history-tour-of-charles-village/)
When: Saturday, 11am-12:30pm
Where: Levering Hall, Homewood Campus
Summary: Join the Diverse Sexuality and Gender Alliance on a walking tour of Charles Village to learn about the LGBTQ+ history of the neighborhood around Homewood Campus.

Heart-Healthy Cooking Demonstration (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/06/hasa-cooking-demonstration/)
When: Saturday, 1-4pm
Where: Charles Commons, Homewood Campus
Summary: Come join Heart and Stroke Awareness at Johns Hopkins and Chef Mario Bush to learn how to prepare a heart-healthy meal.

Fresh Food Cafe's Sterling Brunch (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/07/fresh-food-cafes-sterling-brunch-last-one-of-academic-year/)
When: Sunday, 10am-2pm
Where: Fresh Food Cafe
Summary: Come to Fresh Foods Café to enjoy a delicious brunch and live musical entertainment provided by various performance groups from the Johns Hopkins campus. This is the last Sterling Brunch of the academic year.

Hatch Innovation Conference (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/07/hatch-innovation-conference/)
When: Sunday, 12-4pm
Where: Mudd Hall, Homewood Campus
Summary: The Hatch Innovation Conference, hosted by TCO Labs, Inc., seeks to showcase and empower entrepreneurship and innovation at Johns Hopkins University with speakers, workshops, and startup alley. Students can ask questions of successful entrepreneurs and innovators and network for a job or internship opportunity.

Lectures+Symposiums

Center for Language and Speech Processing Spring Seminar Series: Heng Ji (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/05/center-for-language-and-speech-processing-spring-seminar-series-heng-ji/)
When: Today, 12-1:15pm
Where: Hackerman Hall, Homewood Campus
Summary: Dr. Heng Ji, a professor in Computer Science at Rensselaer Polytechnic Institute, will present a Center for Language and Speech Processing seminar titled "Universal Information Extraction."

8th Annual Racism, Immigration, and Citizenship Conference: "Eyes on Surveillance: (In)security in Everyday Life" (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/05/eighth-annual-jhu-program-in-racism-immigration-and-citizenship-conference-eyes-on-surveillance-insecurity-in-everyday-life/)
When: Today - Saturday, 12-6pm
Where: Homewood Campus
Summary: The theme of the interdisciplinary 8th Annual Johns Hopkins University Program in Racism, Immigration, and Citizenship Conference is "Eyes on Surveillance: (In)security in Everyday Life." This year's conference interrogates the normalization of surveillance in our everyday life.

Latin America in a Globalizing World Seminar: Jorge Coronado (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/05/lagw-seminar-selling-lo-andino-globally-cultural-consumption-and-local-production-in-the-work-of-elena-izcue-by-jorge-coronado/)
When: Today, 12-1pm
Where: Gilman Hall, Homewood Campus
Summary: Jorge Coronado, a professor of modern Latin American and Andean literatures and cultures at Northwestern University, will give a talk entitled "Selling Lo Andino Globally: Cultural Consumption and Local Production in the Work of Elena Izcue" for the Latin America in a Globalizing World project.

Division of Health Sciences Informatics Grand Rounds: Dr. Muhammad Abdul-Mageed (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/05/dhsi-grand-rounds-advances-in-deep-representation-learning-and-applications-to-health/)
When: Today, 12:45-1:45pm
Where: East Baltimore Campus
Summary: Dr. Muhammad Abdul-Mageed, an assistant professor at the University of British Columbia's iSchool, cross appointed with the Department of Linguistics, and an associate member of the Department of Computer Science, will give a talk entitled "Advances in Deep Representation Learning and Applications to Health" as part of the Division of Health Sciences Informatics's Grand Rounds.

The 2019 Gary Laben Lecture featuring Dr. Kathleen Allen (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/05/the-2019-gary-laben-lecture-featuring-dr-kathleen-allen/)
When: Today, 1:30-2:45pm
Where: Gilman Hall, Homewood Campus
Summary: Join the Center for Leadership Education for the 2019 Gary Laben Lecture featuring Dr. Kathleen Allen, president of Kathleen Allen and Associates. Dr. Allen's lecture, titled "Leading from the Roots: Leading Living Systems," will provide valuable insight for those interested in learning more about leadership through the lens of living systems thinking.

Center for Environmental and Applied Fluid Mechanics Seminar: Pavlos P. Vlachos (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/05/center-for-environmental-and-applied-fluid-mechanics-ceafm-seminar-pavlos-vlachos/)
When: Today, 3-4pm
Where: Gilman Hall, Homewood Campus
Summary: The Center for Environmental and Applied Fluid Mechanics presents Dr. Pavlos P. Vlachos, a professor of Mechanical Engineering at Purdue University, who will discuss "Clinical Flow Measurements Putting Experimental Fluid Mechanics in Clinical Practice." This seminar is hosted by Dr. Rui Ni, an assistant professor of Mechanical Engineering at Johns Hopkins University.

Women in STEM Symposium 2019 (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/06/women-in-stem-symposium-2019/)
When: Saturday, 8am-5pm
Where: Turner Concourse, East Baltimore Campus
Summary: Women of Whiting is excited to host a one-day symposium filled with interactive workshops, poster sessions, and networking opportunities.

Hop Talks: An Alumni Panel Discussion featuring Dr. Zeid Ra'ad (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/06/hop-talks-an-alumni-panel-discussion-featuring-dr-zeid-raad-former-united-nations-high-commission/)
When: Saturday, 10:30am-12pm
Where: Hodson Hall, Homewood Campus
Summary: Dr. Steven David, a professor of Political Science, will moderate a thoughtful conversation of alumni panelists, including alumnus Dr. Zeid Ra'ad (Class of 1987) who served on the United Nations High Commissioner for Human Rights. The panel will discuss human rights, the role of the UN, and the state of the world.

Billie Holiday Celebration with Lawrence Jackson and Candice Hoyes (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/06/billie-holiday-celebration-w-lawrence-jackson-candice-hoyes/)
When: Saturday, 7-8pm
Where: Motor House Baltimore, 120 W North Ave, Baltimore, MD 21201
Summary: Dr. Lawrence Jackson, a professor of English and History at Johns Hopkins University, will deliver a public lecture at Motor House in conjunction with several events celebrating the life and music of jazz singer Billie Holiday. Jackson's presentation is a story map exploring Billie Holiday's early life in Baltimore City, conveyed on the ArcGIS [Geospatial Information Service] database.

Biophysics Seminar: Peter Bolhuis (https://hub.jhu.edu/events/2019/04/08/biophysics-seminar-peter-bolhuis/)
When: Monday, 12-1pm
Where: Bloomberg Center, Homewood Campus
Summary: The Johns Hopkins Biophysics Department presents Dr. Peter Bolhuis, a professor in the Van 't Hoff Institute for Molecular Sciences at the University of Amsterdam, who will give a seminar titled "Bridging Length and Time Scales in Biomolecular Systems and Biochemical Networks."

.F Hopkins Groups <relay@relay.engage.campuslabs.com>
.S Homecoming Game - Student Entry Policy
.M
Good afternoon -

As you are putting your alumni weekend plans together, we wanted to remind you of the Men’s Lacrosse student entry policy change.

Blue Jay Rewards is now your official ticket link for the game. All Homewood students must have the Blue Jay Rewards app to enter the game. Follow the easy steps below to get your tickets today and have fun cheering on your Blue Jays!

1. Download Blue Jay Rewards free on your android or iPhone by searching “Blue Jay Rewards”

2. Create/Update your account
                a) Register the app using your JHU email address. For example, John Doe would register under jdoe1@jhu.edu<mailto:jdoe1@jhu.edu>
                b) If you are already registered on the app, go under “Account” > “Edit Profile” and make sure your account is registered under your JHU email address

3. On Gameday, go to the “Gameday Entry” module and you will see a QR code and a link for the game tickets. Do not click this link.

4. Enter Homewood Field via the Student Entrance

5. Show the ticket staff your gameday link, which they will select for you.

6. Cheer on the Blue Jays!


Vina Ranaivoarijaona​

Johns Hopkins University
